<meta name="DC.Description" content="Parasitic plants form a specialized organ, a haustorium, to invade host tissues and acquire water and nutrients. To understand the molecular mechanism of haustorium development, we performed a forward genetics screening to isolate mutants exhibiting haustorial defects in the model parasitic plant Phtheirospermum japonicum. We isolated two mutants that show prolonged and sometimes aberrant meristematic activity in the haustorium apex, resulting in severe defects on host invasion. Whole-genome sequencing revealed that the two mutants respectively have point mutations in homologs of ETHYLENE RESPONSE 1 ( ETR1 ) and ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE 2 ( EIN2 ), signaling components in response to the gaseous phytohormone ethylene. Application of the ethylene signaling inhibitors also caused similar haustorial defects, indicating that ethylene signaling regulates cell proliferation and differentiation of parasite cells. Genetic disruption of host ethylene production also perturbs parasite invasion. We propose that parasitic plants use ethylene as a signal to invade host roots." />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Parasitic plants form a specialized organ, a haustorium, to invade host tissues and acquire water and nutrients. To understand the molecular mechanism of haustorium development, we performed a forward genetics screening to isolate mutants exhibiting haustorial defects in the model parasitic plant &lt;i&gt;Phtheirospermum japonicum.&lt;/i&gt; We isolated two mutants that show prolonged and sometimes aberrant meristematic activity in the haustorium apex, resulting in severe defects on host invasion. Whole-genome sequencing revealed that the two mutants respectively have point mutations in homologs of &lt;i&gt;ETHYLENE RESPONSE 1&lt;/i&gt; (&lt;i&gt;ETR1&lt;/i&gt;) and &lt;i&gt;ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE 2&lt;/i&gt; (&lt;i&gt;EIN2&lt;/i&gt;), signaling components in response to the gaseous phytohormone ethylene. Application of the ethylene signaling inhibitors also caused similar haustorial defects, indicating that ethylene signaling regulates cell proliferation and differentiation of parasite cells. Genetic disruption of host ethylene production also perturbs parasite invasion. We propose that parasitic plants use ethylene as a signal to invade host roots.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="citation_fulltext_world_readable" content="" />
<meta name="twitter:title" content="Ethylene signaling mediates host invasion by parasitic plants" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/6/44/eabc2385/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Parasitic plants form a specialized organ, a haustorium, to invade host tissues and acquire water and nutrients. To understand the molecular mechanism of haustorium development, we performed a forward genetics screening to isolate mutants exhibiting haustorial defects in the model parasitic plant Phtheirospermum japonicum. We isolated two mutants that show prolonged and sometimes aberrant meristematic activity in the haustorium apex, resulting in severe defects on host invasion. Whole-genome sequencing revealed that the two mutants respectively have point mutations in homologs of ETHYLENE RESPONSE 1 ( ETR1 ) and ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE 2 ( EIN2 ), signaling components in response to the gaseous phytohormone ethylene. Application of the ethylene signaling inhibitors also caused similar haustorial defects, indicating that ethylene signaling regulates cell proliferation and differentiation of parasite cells. Genetic disruption of host ethylene production also perturbs parasite invasion. We propose that parasitic plants use ethylene as a signal to invade host roots." />
<meta name="og-title" property="og:title" content="Ethylene signaling mediates host invasion by parasitic plants" />
<meta name="og-url" property="og:url" content="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/6/44/eabc2385" />
<meta name="og-site-name" property="og:site_name" content="Science Advances" />
<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Parasitic plants form a specialized organ, a haustorium, to invade host tissues and acquire water and nutrients. To understand the molecular mechanism of haustorium development, we performed a forward genetics screening to isolate mutants exhibiting haustorial defects in the model parasitic plant Phtheirospermum japonicum. We isolated two mutants that show prolonged and sometimes aberrant meristematic activity in the haustorium apex, resulting in severe defects on host invasion. Whole-genome sequencing revealed that the two mutants respectively have point mutations in homologs of ETHYLENE RESPONSE 1 ( ETR1 ) and ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE 2 ( EIN2 ), signaling components in response to the gaseous phytohormone ethylene. Application of the ethylene signaling inhibitors also caused similar haustorial defects, indicating that ethylene signaling regulates cell proliferation and differentiation of parasite cells. Genetic disruption of host ethylene production also perturbs parasite invasion. We propose that parasitic plants use ethylene as a signal to invade host roots." />

    <script id="loadcss">
      /*! CSS rel=preload polyfill. Depends on loadCSS function. [c]2016 @scottjehl, Filament Group, Inc. Licensed MIT  */
(function( w ){
  // rel=preload support test
  if( !w.loadCSS ){
    return;
  }
  var rp = loadCSS.relpreload = {};
  rp.support = function(){
    try {
      return w.document.createElement( "link" ).relList.supports( "preload" );
    } catch (e) {
      return false;
    }
  };

  // loop preload links and fetch using loadCSS
  rp.poly = function(){
    var links = w.document.getElementsByTagName( "link" );
    for( var i = 0; i < links.length; i++ ){
      var link = links[ i ];
      if( link.rel === "preload" && link.getAttribute( "as" ) === "style" ){
        w.loadCSS( link.href, link );
        link.rel = null;
      }
    }
  };

  // if link[rel=preload] is not supported, we must fetch the CSS manually using loadCSS
  if( !rp.support() ){
    rp.poly();
    var run = w.setInterval( rp.poly, 300 );
    if( w.addEventListener ){
      w.addEventListener( "load", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } );
    }
    if( w.attachEvent ){
      w.attachEvent( "onload", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } )
    }
  }
}( this ));

<div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container article__body">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_sci_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_sci_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" data-highwire-glossary-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-4">Parasitic plants form a specialized organ, a haustorium, to invade host tissues and acquire water and nutrients. To understand the molecular mechanism of haustorium development, we performed a forward genetics screening to isolate mutants exhibiting haustorial defects in the model parasitic plant <em>Phtheirospermum japonicum.</em> We isolated two mutants that show prolonged and sometimes aberrant meristematic activity in the haustorium apex, resulting in severe defects on host invasion. Whole-genome sequencing revealed that the two mutants respectively have point mutations in homologs of <em>ETHYLENE RESPONSE 1</em> (<em>ETR1</em>) and <em>ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE 2</em> (<em>EIN2</em>), signaling components in response to the gaseous phytohormone ethylene. Application of the ethylene signaling inhibitors also caused similar haustorial defects, indicating that ethylene signaling regulates cell proliferation and differentiation of parasite cells. Genetic disruption of host ethylene production also perturbs parasite invasion. We propose that parasitic plants use ethylene as a signal to invade host roots.</p></div><div class="section introduction" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">INTRODUCTION</h2><p id="p-5">Parasitic plants in the Orobanchaceae family include destructive parasitic weeds, such as <em>Striga</em> spp. and <em>Phelipanche</em> spp., but the molecular basis for their parasitism is largely unexplored. Parasitic plants form haustoria to attach and invade hosts through which parasitic plants acquire nutrients as well as small materials including plant hormones and RNAs (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1"><em>1</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3"><em>3</em></a>). Formation of haustoria is regulated by host-derived signal molecules. Many Orobanchaceae members initiate haustorium formation in response to cell wall–derived simple quinones or phenolics, such as 2,6-dimethoxy-<em>p</em>-benzoquinone (DMBQ) or syringic acid, collectively termed haustorium-inducing factors (HIFs) (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1"><em>1</em></a>). In the model Orobanchaceae <em>Phtheirospermum japonicum</em>, HIFs induce the expression of an auxin biosynthesis gene in root epidermal cells at haustorium formation sites, causing cell division and expansion to form a semispherical pre- or early haustorium structure (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4"><em>4</em></a>). To establish a successful parasitic relationship, a haustorium needs to reach the host roots, and upon host contact, it initiates invasion by differentiating epidermal cells at the apex into slender intrusive cells (movie S1) (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6"><em>6</em></a>) that function in host intrusion. After the intrusive cells reach host vasculatures, the haustorium develops water-conducting xylem bridges between host and parasite xylems, forming a mature haustorium (<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5"><em>5</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). When exposed to HIFs without a host in close proximity, haustorium formation is induced, but its growth is soon arrested (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8"><em>8</em></a>). Thus, the fate of haustorium cells is determined by host availability; however, how parasitic plants recognize host availability and how they regulate such a cell fate transition remain unknown.</p><p id="p-6">Ethylene is a gaseous plant hormone contributing important agronomic traits such as fruit ripening, leaf senescence, and root nodulation (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10"><em>10</em></a>). Ethylene is also broadly implicated in plant interaction with many organisms including insects, bacteria, and virus, conferring plants either resistance or susceptibility, depending on pathogen types (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13"><em>13</em></a>). Ethylene biosynthesis and signaling pathways have been well studied in the model plant <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>. The biosynthesis pathway consists of two steps. The first step converts <em>S</em>-adenosyl-<span class="sc">l</span>-methionine to 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid (ACC) by ACC synthase (ACS). The second step releases ethylene from ACC by ACC oxidase (ACO) (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14"><em>14</em></a>). Ethylene is perceived by endoplasmic reticulum (ER) membrane–localized ethylene receptors that have similarity to sensor histidine kinases in the bacterial two-component system (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15"><em>15</em></a>). The <em>Arabidopsis</em> genome contains five ethylene receptors including <em>ETHYLENE RESPONSE 1</em> (<em>ETR1</em>). In the absence of ethylene, ethylene receptors inhibit the key downstream signaling component ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE 2 (EIN2) by activating CONSTITUTIVE TRIPLE RESPONSE 1 (CTR1). CTR1 phosphorylates the C-terminal domain of EIN2, preventing its nuclear translocation that activates downstream ethylene signaling. Without ethylene, EIN2 is constantly targeted for the ubiquitin proteasome–dependent degradation pathway (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15"><em>15</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19"><em>19</em></a>). Upon ethylene binding to ethylene receptors, the activity of CTR1 is suppressed, leading to nuclear translocation of the EIN2 C-terminal domain and activation of the downstream signaling cascade via key transcription factors ETHYLENE INSENSITIVE 3 (EIN3) and EIN3-LIKE 1 (EIL1) (<a id="xref-ref-15-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15"><em>15</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>). In the Orobanchaceae parasitic plant <em>Triphysaria versicolor</em>, it was reported that ethylene production is up-regulated when parasites are exposed to HIFs without a nearby host (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>). Pharmacological perturbation of ethylene biosynthesis or signaling in <em>T. versicolor</em> resulted in reduced haustorium initiation, suggesting the involvement of ethylene in haustorium initiation. However, the precise roles of ethylene in parasitic plant and host interaction remain unknown.</p><p id="p-7">In this study, we identified <em>P. japonicum</em> mutants that cannot regulate cell division and cell fate transitions at the haustorium apex and thus showed defects in host invasion. Genome sequencing revealed that these mutants are impaired in ethylene signaling. Our results suggest that ethylene mediates parasitic plant host recognition for the invasion.</p></div><div class="section results-discussion" id="sec-2"><h2 class="">RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Parasitic plant mutants that show extra haustorium elongation</h3><p id="p-8">With the aim of unveiling the genetic programs for haustorium development, we carried out a genetic screening using the facultative root parasitic plant <em>P. japonicum</em>. Screening of ethyl methanesulfonate (EMS)–induced mutant pools identified two mutant lines that had elongated haustoria on a medium containing DMBQ (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1A</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a>). Time-lapse observations showed delayed growth termination of haustoria in both mutants. Wild-type (WT) haustoria stopped its tip growth within 2 days after DMBQ treatment, probably to suppress unnecessary growth in the absence of a host. In contrast, mutant haustoria kept elongating even after 3 days of DMBQ treatment (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1A</a>), suggesting that the mutants have defects in such a suppression system. Mutant haustoria often produce a single xylem strand in the middle axis (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1, A and B</a>, and fig. S1). The tracheary elements of mutant xylem strands show spiral patterns observed in the protoxylems of roots or a xylem bridge in a mature haustorium (fig. S1, A to D), but these xylems are not directly connected with root xylems unlike those in the lateral root (arrowheads; <a id="xref-fig-1-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1B</a>). The cellular structure of the mutant haustoria is also distinctive from those of roots, indicating that elongated mutants’ haustoria are different from roots (fig. S1, E to P). Each mutant was backcrossed (BC) with a WT parental line, and the subsequently obtained BC1F1 and BC1F2 progeny were phenotypically examined. One of two mutant lines (line 1) showed severe germination defects and, therefore, was highly variable in phenotypic segregation ratios in the BC1F2 progeny. Nevertheless, the germinated BC1F1 plants had the elongated haustorium phenotype, indicating a dominant mutation. On the other hand, BC1F2 progeny of the other mutant (line 2) showed almost a 3:1 (211:65) segregation for WT versus the elongated haustorium phenotype, indicating that the phenotype is caused by a recessive mutation in a single gene. Both mutants required at least 1 week of stratification to induce germination, while the WT required 1-day stratification, indicating that both mutants showed a seed germination phenotype.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F1">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">

  </figure></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>The mutant phenotype is caused by mutations in ethylene signaling genes</h3><p id="p-10">To identify the genes responsible for the phenotype, we sequenced the whole genomes of WT and the mutants and identified single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) unique to each mutant. The WT reference genome was sequenced and assembled, resulting in a draft genome assembly with an N50 scaffold over 1 million base pairs (Mbp) and an N50 contig over 29 kilo–base pairs (kbp). Gene annotation using the <em>P. japonicum</em> transcriptome predicted 30,337 protein encoding genes in the assembly (tables S1 and S2). By Illumina-based resequencing of the pooled mutant genomes, we identified two nonsynonymous SNPs unique to line 2 M5 and BC1F2 progenies (see details in Materials and Methods, table S3, and data S1). One of the SNPs was found in a gene encoding protein phosphatase 2C. Another SNP was found in a gene encoding a homolog of <em>Arabidopsis EIN2</em> (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16"><em>16</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22"><em>22</em></a>), a key signal transducer of ethylene (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11"><em>11</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13"><em>13</em></a>). The <em>P. japonicum EIN2</em> homolog (Pj<em>EIN2</em>) is a single copy gene in the genome and shares 49% amino acid identity with <em>Arabidopsis EIN2</em> (figs. S3 and S4A). The mutation in Pj<em>EIN2</em> causes a stop codon at amino acid 744 by replacing an Arg residue (<a id="xref-fig-1-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1D</a> and fig. S3), likely resulting in partial removal of the C-terminal half of Pj<em>EIN2</em> that is required for activating ethylene signaling in the nucleus (<a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16"><em>16</em></a>).</p><p id="p-11">For the dominant line 1 mutant, we obtained 36 candidates of nonsynonymous SNPs (fig. S5, table S3, and data S2). One of the SNPs occurs in a gene encoding a homolog of <em>Arabidopsis ETHYLENE RECEPTOR1</em> (<em>ETR1</em>) with 80% amino acid identity. The mutation in PjETR1 replaces an Ile residue with Phe within the first of three hydrophobic regions at the N terminus (<a id="xref-fig-1-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>) that function in ethylene binding in ETR1 (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23"><em>23</em></a>). Mutations in these regions in <em>Arabidopsis ETR1</em> result in inability of ETR1 to bind ethylene and lead to dominant ethylene-insensitive phenotype (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24"><em>24</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25"><em>25</em></a>). Similar to a comparable mutant in <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25"><em>25</em></a>), the line 1 mutant exhibits dominant phenotype, although there are multiple ethylene receptor–encoding genes in addition to Pj<em>ETR1</em> (fig. S4B).</p><p id="p-12">Because both mutants have SNPs in homologs of key signaling genes in the ethylene pathway, we assumed that this phenotype may be caused by defects in ethylene signaling in <em>P. japonicum</em>. Consistent with this hypothesis, both mutants were ethylene insensitive for growth inhibition of roots and hypocotyls (fig. S6). Moreover, transformation of a full-length Pj<em>EIN2</em> genomic region restores the haustorium phenotype of the line 2 mutant (<a id="xref-fig-1-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1E</a>), confirming that haustorium elongation is caused by a loss-of-function mutation in Pj<em>EIN2</em>. Therefore, we concluded that the elongated haustorium phenotype is caused by ethylene insensitivity and named these mutants Pj<em>etr1</em> and Pj<em>ein2</em> for lines 1 and 2, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-1-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). Elongated haustoria are also induced by treatments with ethylene signaling inhibitors AgNO<sub>3</sub> and 1-methylcyclopropene (1-MCP) (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26"><em>26</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27"><em>27</em></a>), mimicking the phenotype of Pj<em>etr1</em> and Pj<em>ein2</em> (<a id="xref-fig-1-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1, F and G</a>). The <em>P. japonicum</em> genome contains 11 ACS-encoding and 4 ACO-encoding genes, each of which belongs to well-conserved subgroups except for type III ACO (fig. S8), indicating the conservation of ethylene biosynthesis pathway. In contrast to ethylene signaling inhibitors, no extra elongation was observed by the treatment of ethylene biosynthesis inhibitors including aminoethoxyvinylglycine (AVG) that inhibits <em>ACSs</em> and aminoisobutyric acid (AIB) that inhibits ACOs (fig. S7). Lack of promotion of haustorium elongation by ethylene biosynthesis inhibitors suggests either that ethylene biosynthesis in <em>P. japonicum</em> is not involved in this process or that residual ethylene amounts still produced under inhibitor treatments are sufficient for stopping haustorial elongation. AgNO<sub>3</sub> treatment induced elongated haustoria similarly in <em>Striga hermonthica</em>, a close relative of <em>P. japonicum</em> (fig. S9). Ethylene biosynthesis genes and receptors are also conserved in <em>Striga asiatica</em> (figs. S4 and S8) (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28"><em>28</em></a>). These results suggest that the function of ethylene signaling in haustorium elongation is conserved in the Orobanchaceae.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Effects of ethylene on haustorium initiation</h3><p id="p-13">Defects in ethylene signaling have little effect on haustorial initiation, as neither treatments with AgNO<sub>3</sub> or 1-MCP nor the <em>Pjetr1</em> or <em>Pjein2</em> mutations affected haustorium number statistical significance, although Pj<em>ein2</em> produced slightly fewer haustoria, indicating that ethylene signaling is not required for haustorium initiation (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2, A to C</a>). On the other hand, application of exogenous ethylene strongly reduced the haustorium number and haustorium size only in the WT (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2, D to F</a>). Note that this suppressive effect is almost saturated at 0.01 parts per million (ppm) ethylene application, while most of other plants show ethylene response that saturates at several magnitude higher concentration, e.g., inhibition of root elongation in <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29"><em>29</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30"><em>30</em></a>). These suggest that <em>P. japonicum</em> is highly sensitive to ethylene at root transition zone where the haustorium emerges and that ethylene strongly suppresses haustorium initiation in an ethylene signaling–dependent manner. AVG treatment inhibiting production of ethylene precursor ACC significantly reduced haustorium number in <em>P. japonicum</em> WT as well as Pj<em>etr1</em> and Pj<em>ein2</em> mutants (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2G</a>), while the same concentration did not affect primary root growth (fig. S10A), indicating that AVG inhibits haustorium initiation independently of PjETR1 and PjEIN2. In <em>T. versicolor</em>, AVG similarly inhibits haustorium induction and suboptimal concentration of ACC increases haustorium formation frequency (<a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>). Therefore, ACC as a precursor of ethylene might have a different role from ethylene and is required for haustorium formation. In line with this, ethylene signaling–independent function of ACC is known in several processes including pollen tube attraction, seed viability, and vegetative growth, proposing that ACC itself might act as a hormone (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31"><em>31</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33"><em>33</em></a>). Alternatively, reduced haustorium initiation may be a consequence of auxin biosynthesis inhibition by AVG. It was reported that AVG inhibits auxin biosynthesis in ethylene-independent manner (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34"><em>34</em></a>) and local auxin biosynthesis is essential for haustorium initiation (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4"><em>4</em></a>). Together, these data collectively led us to conclude that while exogenous application of ethylene suppressed haustorium development, ethylene signaling is dispensable during early haustorium initiation but is required for timely termination of haustorium growth in the absence of a host.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F2">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Roles of ethylene and ethylene signaling on haustorium initiation. (A) Number of haustoria in the WT (n = 24), Pjetr1 (n = 14), and Pjein2 (n = 19) after DMBQ treatment for 1 week. (B and C) Number of WT haustoria after DMBQ treatment for 1 week with or without AgNO3 (B) or 1-MCP (C). Data represent the mean &#xB1; SE. n &#x2265; 14. (D) WT P. japonicum and the Pjetr1 and Pjein2 mutants were treated with 10 &#x3BC;M DMBQ in the absence or presence of 0.1 ppm ethylene for 7 days. Top panels show macro images of plant roots, and the bottom panels show magnified images of root segments with haustoria (yellow arrowheads). Scale bars, 1 cm (top) and 0.5 cm (bottom). (E) Magnified image of haustorium induced by 10 &#x3BC;M DMBQ in the absence or presence of 0.1 ppm ethylene. (F) Relative number of haustoria in P. japonicum WT, Pjetr1, and Pjein2 after treatment with 10 &#x3BC;M DMBQ for 7 days in the absence or presence of ethylene at the indicated concentrations (ppm). (G) Relative number of haustoria after treatment with 10 &#x3BC;M DMBQ for 7 days in the absence or presence of AVG at the indicated concentrations (&#x3BC;M). Data indicate the relative mean &#xB1; SE. Student&#x2019;s t test, unpaired, two-tailed (*P &lt; 0.01). n &#x2265; 13 from two independent experiments. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-884174781" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 2&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;Roles of ethylene and ethylene signaling on haustorium initiation.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;p id=&quot;p-14&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;(&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) Number of haustoria in the WT (&lt;em&gt;n&lt;/em&gt; = 24), Pj&lt;em&gt;etr1&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;n&lt;/em&gt; = 14), and Pj&lt;em&gt;ein2&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;n&lt;/em&gt; = 19) after DMBQ treatment for 1 week. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F2.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F2.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">

  </figure></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Ethylene signaling negatively regulates cell proliferation and auxin response in haustorial apex</h3><p id="p-15">Apical growth in plants, such as root or shoot growth, is often driven by cell division at the apex wherein the plant hormone auxin plays a key role in maintaining cell division (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35"><em>35</em></a>). To test whether haustorial apical growth is regulated by similar mechanisms, we observed cell division during haustorium development. A cluster of small cells with darker toluidine blue staining at the haustorium apex was observed up to 2 days after DMBQ treatment in WT, whereas these cells are observed until 3 days in the Pj<em>etr1</em> and Pj<em>ein2</em> mutants (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3A</a>). Visualization of cell proliferation by 5-ethynyl-2′-deoxyuridine (EdU) incorporation into newly synthesized DNA confirms that haustorium apex cells are proliferated for up to 2 days in WT, whereas cell proliferation activity remains up to 4 days in the Pj<em>ein2</em> mutant (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3B</a>). In addition, cell proliferation in Pj<em>ein2</em> occurs in the central parts of haustoria coinciding with haustorial xylem formation, indicating that vascular meristems may develop in mutant haustoria (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3B</a>). Expression of the auxin-responsive promoter DR5 coincides with haustorial apical cell proliferation, i.e., there was prolonged occurrence of auxin response maxima in Pj<em>ein2</em> haustorium apices in Pj<em>ein2</em> compared to WT (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3C</a>). Failure in refining haustorium apex activity sometimes leads to formation of multiple tips within a single haustorium in Pj<em>ein2</em> induced by either <em>Arabidopsis</em> roots or syringic acid (<a id="xref-fig-3-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3D</a>), a precursor of DMBQ. These data suggest that ethylene signaling is necessary to terminate cell proliferation at the haustorium apex via suppression of the auxin response.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F3">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Ethylene signaling regulates cell division at the haustorium apex. Scale bars, 50 &#x3BC;m (A), 200 &#x3BC;m (B and C), and 500 &#x3BC;m (D).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F3.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F3.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">

  </figure></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Ethylene signaling is essential for host invasion</h3><p id="p-17">To determine the role of ethylene signaling during host invasion, we performed an infection assay using <em>Arabidopsis</em> and rice (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). Most of the haustoria in WT successfully invade host roots at levels of 82 ± 7% and 85 ± 4% against <em>Arabidopsis</em> and rice, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4, B and E</a>). However, host invasion levels were significantly reduced in Pj<em>etr1</em> (compromised 65 to 70%) and, to a greater extent, in Pj<em>ein2</em> (91 to 98%) (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4, B and E</a>). Noninvaded haustoria of both mutants failed to develop intrusive cells at the apex and kept elongating around the host surface even after direct contact with <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a id="xref-fig-4-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4, A, C, and D</a>). In the interaction with rice, most of the Pj<em>etr1</em> haustoria were arrested at the root surface, resulting in swelling at the haustorial tip without forming intrusive cells (“Blocked” in <a id="xref-fig-4-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4D</a>), probably due to the thicker roots of rice compared with those of <em>Arabidopsis</em>. However, most haustoria in Pj<em>ein2</em> passed around the rice roots without stopping apex cell division, as seen in <em>Arabidopsis</em> infection. These observations indicate that ethylene signaling in the parasite is crucial for the haustorium apex cells to differentiate into intrusive cells for host invasion.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F4">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Mutations in ethylene signaling components in parasitic plants cause severe defects in host infection. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-884174781" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 4&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;Mutations in ethylene signaling components in parasitic plants cause severe defects in host infection.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;p id=&quot;p-18&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;(&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) Haustoria of &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; WT, Pj&lt;em&gt;etr1&lt;/em&gt;, and Pj&lt;em&gt;ein2&lt;/em&gt; infecting &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; roots for 5 days. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F4-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F4.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F4-caption" src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F4.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">

  </figure></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Parasitic plants use host ethylene for invasion</h3><p id="p-19">Next, we examined whether parasite- or host-derived ethylene contributes to invasion. Consistent to the mutant phenotypes, application of the ethylene signaling inhibitor AgNO<sub>3</sub> completely suppressed the host invasion (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5, A and B</a>). Application of ethylene biosynthesis inhibitors AVG and AIB reduced frequency of successful haustorium invasion, suggesting that ethylene production in either the parasite or host is involved in invasion processes (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5, A and B</a>). Because the same concentration of these inhibitors did not inhibit primary root growth (fig. S10B), the reduced invasion levels by these inhibitors are not due to toxicity of the treatment. Unlike AgNO<sub>3</sub>, AVG- and AIB-treated haustoria stopped their elongation at the host root surface (<a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5A</a>), suggesting that stoppage of haustorium apex cell division and differentiation to intrusive cells can be uncoupled, although both processes are mediated by ethylene signaling. Invasion by <em>P. japonicum</em> was 35% lower when infecting the <em>Arabidopsis</em> ethylene biosynthesis mutant defective in seven isoforms of <em>ACSs</em> (<em>heptuple</em>) (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32"><em>32</em></a>) than when infecting WT <em>Arabidopsis</em>. (<a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5C</a>). <em>Arabidopsis etr1</em> and <em>ein2</em>, but not constitutively active ethylene signaling <em>ctr1</em>, were also less invaded (<a id="xref-fig-5-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5C</a>) to a similar level as the <em>heptuple</em> ACS mutant. These may indicate that the host ethylene signaling is required for parasite invasion. Alternatively, excess amount of ethylene production in hosts may prevent parasite invasion. It was previously shown that ethylene production in <em>Arabidopsis</em> is enhanced in <em>etr1</em> and <em>ein2</em> due to the negative feedback regulation of ethylene biosynthesis (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36"><em>36</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37"><em>37</em></a>). Ethylene-overproducing mutant <em>eto1</em> exhibited 15% lower invasion rate than did the WT, although not significantly. The reduced invasion rate might be associated with the negative role of exogenous ethylene on haustorium development as shown in <a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2 (D to F)</a>. These suggest that very localized ethylene production of host and its perception in parasite could be essential for invasion.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F5">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Host ethylene production and signaling contribute to parasite invasion. (A) Representative images of noninvaded haustoria of WT P. japonicum on WT Arabidopsis in the absence (control) or presence of ethylene biosynthesis (AVG and AIB) and signaling inhibitors (AgNO3) after 3 days of infection. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m. (B) Invasion level of WT P. japonicum haustoria on WT Arabidopsis in the presence of ethylene biosynthesis and signaling inhibitors after 3 days of infection. Data represent the mean &#xB1; SE (n &#x2265; 14) from three independent experiments. (C) Invasion level of WT P. japonicum haustoria on Arabidopsis WT and the ethylene biosynthesis (heptuple), ethylene overproducing (eto1), ethylene signaling (etr1 and ein2), or constitutively activated ethylene signaling (ctr1) mutants after 3 days of infection (n &#x2265; 27). AVG, 5 &#x3BC;M; AIB, 1 mM; AgNO3, 0.1 &#x3BC;M (*P &lt; 0.05 and **P &lt; 0.01, Student&#x2019;s t test, unpaired, two-tailed, compared to control or WT)." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-884174781" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 5&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;Host ethylene production and signaling contribute to parasite invasion.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;p id=&quot;p-20&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;(&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) Representative images of noninvaded haustoria of WT &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; on WT &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; in the absence (control) or presence of ethylene biosynthesis (AVG and AIB) and signaling inhibitors (AgNO&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;) after 3 days of infection. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m. (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt;) Invasion level of WT &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; haustoria on WT &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; in the presence of ethylene biosynthesis and signaling inhibitors after 3 days of infection. Data represent the mean &#xB1; SE (&lt;em&gt;n&lt;/em&gt; &#x2265; 14) from three independent experiments. (&lt;strong&gt;C&lt;/strong&gt;) Invasion level of WT &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; haustoria on &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt; WT and the ethylene biosynthesis (&lt;em&gt;heptuple&lt;/em&gt;), ethylene overproducing (&lt;em&gt;eto1&lt;/em&gt;), ethylene signaling (&lt;em&gt;etr1&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;ein2&lt;/em&gt;), or constitutively activated ethylene signaling (&lt;em&gt;ctr1&lt;/em&gt;) mutants after 3 days of infection (&lt;em&gt;n&lt;/em&gt; &#x2265; 27). AVG, 5 &#x3BC;M; AIB, 1 mM; AgNO&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;, 0.1 &#x3BC;M (*&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &lt; 0.05 and **&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &lt; 0.01, Student&#x2019;s &lt;em&gt;t&lt;/em&gt; test, unpaired, two-tailed, compared to control or WT).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F5-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F5.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F5-caption" src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F5.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 5"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/241910" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F5-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 5</span> <span class="caption-title">Host ethylene production and signaling contribute to parasite invasion.</span><p id="p-20" class="first-child">(<strong>A</strong>) Representative images of noninvaded haustoria of WT <em>P. japonicum</em> on WT <em>Arabidopsis</em> in the absence (control) or presence of ethylene biosynthesis (AVG and AIB) and signaling inhibitors (AgNO<sub>3</sub>) after 3 days of infection. Scale bars, 200 μm. (<strong>B</strong>) Invasion level of WT <em>P. japonicum</em> haustoria on WT <em>Arabidopsis</em> in the presence of ethylene biosynthesis and signaling inhibitors after 3 days of infection. Data represent the mean ± SE (<em>n</em> ≥ 14) from three independent experiments. (<strong>C</strong>) Invasion level of WT <em>P. japonicum</em> haustoria on <em>Arabidopsis</em> WT and the ethylene biosynthesis (<em>heptuple</em>), ethylene overproducing (<em>eto1</em>), ethylene signaling (<em>etr1</em> and <em>ein2</em>), or constitutively activated ethylene signaling (<em>ctr1</em>) mutants after 3 days of infection (<em>n</em> ≥ 27). AVG, 5 μM; AIB, 1 mM; AgNO<sub>3</sub>, 0.1 μM (*<em>P</em> &lt; 0.05 and **<em>P</em> &lt; 0.01, Student’s <em>t</em> test, unpaired, two-tailed, compared to control or WT).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Haustorium apex cell regulation to archive successful invasion</h3><p id="p-21">Why do parasitic plants regulate haustorium apex growth and cell differentiation using ethylene signaling? We postulate that parasitic plants use this system to reach the root surface of hosts to start invasion at the precise point. Root extracts of <em>Arabidopsis</em> and rice occasionally induce long haustoria in WT <em>P. japonicum</em> (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6A</a>). Elongated haustoria are also observed when a <em>P. japonicum</em> root is placed on the same agar plate in close proximity to a host, indicating that diffusible signals from the host promote haustorium elongation. These elongated haustoria can eventually infect hosts when they reach hosts (<a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6A</a>). Exogenous application of ethylene prevented haustorium elongation induced by host root extracts, indicating that ethylene is a suppressor for haustorium elongation (<a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6, B and C</a>). Thus, parasitic plants initiate haustorium development when they detect HIFs and maintain haustorial apex growth until they locate the exact position where a host produces ethylene (<a id="xref-fig-6-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6D</a>).</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F6">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Haustorium elongation in WT P. japonicum in response to host root extracts or exudates. (A) Longest haustorium of WT P. japonicum after a 10-day treatment with DMBQ, syringic acid, root extracts, or root exudates of rice and Arabidopsis. Concentrations of chemicals and extracts are indicated. For host root infection, the roots of WT P. japonicum were placed at a distance from the host roots on agar medium, allowing haustoria elongation and infection. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m. (B) Representative images of haustoria in the WT P. japonicum in rice extract treatment followed by ethylene application. Haustoria were induced by 1% rice root extracts for 1 day, and the samples were subsequently placed in cambers containing 0, 0.001, or 0.01 ppm ethylene for 7 days. DMBQ (10 &#x3BC;M) was used as control. (C) Lengths of the longest haustorium in each plant from the experiment in (B). Data represent the mean &#xB1; SE (n &#x2265; 11) (*P &lt; 0.05, Student&#x2019;s t test, unpaired, two-tailed, compared to DMBQ treatment). (D) A model for the roles of parasitic plant ethylene signaling in the regulation of haustorium fate. Upon exposure to HIFs, the haustorium initiates and develops through division of apex cells. When there is no host available for attachment, ethylene (ET) signaling in parasitic plants suppresses the proliferation of haustorium apex cells to terminate haustorium growth. Upon host attachment, parasitic plant ethylene signaling is crucial for differentiation of the haustorium apex cells into intrusive cells to allow host invasion. Ethylene biosynthesis and signaling pathways of the host contribute to susceptibility to parasitic plants." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-884174781" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 6&lt;/span&gt; &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;Haustorium elongation in WT &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; in response to host root extracts or exudates.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;p id=&quot;p-22&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;(&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) Longest haustorium of WT &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; after a 10-day treatment with DMBQ, syringic acid, root extracts, or root exudates of rice and &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/em&gt;. Concentrations of chemicals and extracts are indicated. For host root infection, the roots of WT &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; were placed at a distance from the host roots on agar medium, allowing haustoria elongation and infection. Scale bars, 200 &#x3BC;m. (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt;) Representative images of haustoria in the WT &lt;em&gt;P. japonicum&lt;/em&gt; in rice extract treatment followed by ethylene application. Haustoria were induced by 1% rice root extracts for 1 day, and the samples were subsequently placed in cambers containing 0, 0.001, or 0.01 ppm ethylene for 7 days. DMBQ (10 &#x3BC;M) was used as control. (&lt;strong&gt;C&lt;/strong&gt;) Lengths of the longest haustorium in each plant from the experiment in (B). Data represent the mean &#xB1; SE (&lt;em&gt;n&lt;/em&gt; &#x2265; 11) (*&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &lt; 0.05, Student&#x2019;s &lt;em&gt;t&lt;/em&gt; test, unpaired, two-tailed, compared to DMBQ treatment). (&lt;strong&gt;D&lt;/strong&gt;) A model for the roles of parasitic plant ethylene signaling in the regulation of haustorium fate. Upon exposure to HIFs, the haustorium initiates and develops through division of apex cells. When there is no host available for attachment, ethylene (ET) signaling in parasitic plants suppresses the proliferation of haustorium apex cells to terminate haustorium growth. Upon host attachment, parasitic plant ethylene signaling is crucial for differentiation of the haustorium apex cells into intrusive cells to allow host invasion. Ethylene biosynthesis and signaling pathways of the host contribute to susceptibility to parasitic plants.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F6-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F6.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F6-caption" src="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F6.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://advances.sciencemag.org/content/advances/6/44/eabc2385/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 6"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>

<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/241998" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F6-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 6</span> <span class="caption-title">Haustorium elongation in WT <em>P. japonicum</em> in response to host root extracts or exudates.</span><p id="p-22" class="first-child">(<strong>A</strong>) Longest haustorium of WT <em>P. japonicum</em> after a 10-day treatment with DMBQ, syringic acid, root extracts, or root exudates of rice and <em>Arabidopsis</em>. Concentrations of chemicals and extracts are indicated. For host root infection, the roots of WT <em>P. japonicum</em> were placed at a distance from the host roots on agar medium, allowing haustoria elongation and infection. Scale bars, 200 μm. (<strong>B</strong>) Representative images of haustoria in the WT <em>P. japonicum</em> in rice extract treatment followed by ethylene application. Haustoria were induced by 1% rice root extracts for 1 day, and the samples were subsequently placed in cambers containing 0, 0.001, or 0.01 ppm ethylene for 7 days. DMBQ (10 μM) was used as control. (<strong>C</strong>) Lengths of the longest haustorium in each plant from the experiment in (B). Data represent the mean ± SE (<em>n</em> ≥ 11) (*<em>P</em> &lt; 0.05, Student’s <em>t</em> test, unpaired, two-tailed, compared to DMBQ treatment). (<strong>D</strong>) A model for the roles of parasitic plant ethylene signaling in the regulation of haustorium fate. Upon exposure to HIFs, the haustorium initiates and develops through division of apex cells. When there is no host available for attachment, ethylene (ET) signaling in parasitic plants suppresses the proliferation of haustorium apex cells to terminate haustorium growth. Upon host attachment, parasitic plant ethylene signaling is crucial for differentiation of the haustorium apex cells into intrusive cells to allow host invasion. Ethylene biosynthesis and signaling pathways of the host contribute to susceptibility to parasitic plants.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><p id="p-23">This study represents the first identification of parasitic plant genes mediating host invasion using forward genetics. Our findings reveal how a parasitic plant uses the ethylene molecule to fine-tune haustorium development and host invasion. Note that in <em>Striga</em> genus, which relies on host-derived strigolactones for germination, ethylene production and signaling play important roles in seed germination at downstream of strigolactone signaling (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38"><em>38</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39"><em>39</em></a>). Exogenous ethylene application was used for suicidal germination to eradicate <em>S. asiatica</em> in the United States invaded in the 1950s (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40"><em>40</em></a>). The severe germination defects in Pj<em>etr1</em> and Pj<em>ein2</em> mutants confirmed that ethylene is also involved in germination of a parasitic species whose germination does not rely on strigolactones. Together, it appears that parasitic plants have co-opted the ethylene signaling for the parasitism at multiple steps of their life cycle, i.e., germination, haustorium growth termination, and host invasion. These knowledge expand the potential use of ethylene and ethylene inhibitors to control a wider range of parasitic weeds including facultative parasites by manipulating haustorial function.</p></div></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-10"><h2 class="">MATERIALS AND METHODS</h2><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Plant materials and growth condition</h3><p id="p-24"><em>P. japonicum</em> (Thunb.) Kanitz ecotype Okayama was used as WT (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41"><em>41</em></a>). The mutants Pj<em>etr1</em> and Pj<em>ein2</em> were isolated from M2 seed pools of EMS-mutagenized <em>P. japonicum</em> as previously described (<a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a>). <em>Arabidopsis</em> ecotype Columbia (Col-0) accession was used as <em>Arabidopsis</em> WT. <em>Arabidopsis heptuple</em> (<em>acs1-1acs2-1acs4-1acs5-2acs6-1acs7-1acs9-1</em>) (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32"><em>32</em></a>), <em>ein2-</em>1 (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42"><em>42</em></a>), <em>etr1-3</em> (<a id="xref-ref-15-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15"><em>15</em></a>), and <em>ctr1</em> (<a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>) mutants were published previously. The <em>heptuple</em> mutant (CS16650) was obtained from the Salk Institute, and <em>etr1-3</em> and <em>ctr1</em> were gifts from Y. Saijo and K. Hiruma at the Nara Institute of Science and Technology. Rice used in this study was <em>Oryza sativa</em> L. subspecies <em>japonica</em>, cv. Koshihikari. <em>S. hermonthica</em> (Del.) Benth seeds were provided by A. G. T. Babiker (Sudan). Surface sterilization of <em>P. japonicum</em>, <em>S. hermonthica</em>, rice, and <em>Arabidopsis</em> seeds was described previously (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43"><em>43</em></a>). Surface-sterilized <em>Arabidopsis</em> seeds were sown on one-half-strength Murashige and Skoog (MS) medium (Wako Chemicals) with 1% (w/v) sucrose and 0.8% (w/v) agar and incubated at 4°C for 1 to 2 days in the dark before transferring the dishes to 25°C. The WT, Pj<em>etr1</em>, and Pj<em>ein2 P. japonicum</em> seeds were sown on one-half-strength MS medium with 1% (w/v) sucrose and 0.8% (w/v) agar and incubated at 4°C for at least 1 week. The <em>P. japonicum</em> seeds were germinated under dark conditions for 3 days at 25°C and grown at 25°C with a photoperiod of 16-hour light (100 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) and 8-hour dark. <em>Arabidopsis</em> seedlings were grown at 22°C under continuous light conditions. Surface-sterilized rice seeds were germinated and grown on a sterile filter paper in 9-cm-diameter petri dishes filled with 15-ml sterile water at 25°C with a photoperiod of 16-hour light and 8-hour dark. Surface-sterilized <em>S. hermonthica</em> seeds were preconditioned and germinated in the dark at 25°C as described previously (<a id="xref-ref-43-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43"><em>43</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>ACC, ethephon, and ethylene treatments</h3><p id="p-25">To investigate <em>P. japonicum</em> root responses to ethephon and ACC, the surface-sterilized seeds were directly sown on growth medium supplemented with ethephon (Wako Chemicals) or ACC (Sigma-Aldrich) at the indicated concentrations and grown vertically in the dark for 3 days. For ethylene application, 10-day-old <em>P. japonicum</em> seedlings grown on growth medium in square petri dishes were treated with ethylene gas treatment. Two small holes were made in the lids of the petri dishes containing plants, and the holes and sides of the petri dishes were sealed with surgical tape to ensure air flow. The petri dishes were placed vertically inside a 9-liter plastic container, and ethylene gas (Japan Fine Products) was injected directly into the container. Root tips before and after ethylene treatment were marked, and root growth was observed with a stereomicroscope (Zeiss Stemi 2000-C).</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Haustorium induction assay</h3><p id="p-26">The haustorium induction procedure and preparation of host root extracts were described previously (<a id="xref-ref-43-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43"><em>43</em></a>). Briefly, 1-week-old <em>P. japonicum</em> seedlings were transferred to nutrient-free 0.8% (w/v) agar medium for starvation treatment for 3 days. Subsequently, seedlings were transferred to 0.8% (w/v) agar medium containing DMBQ (Sigma-Aldrich) with or without ethylene inhibitors and grown vertically for 7 or 10 days for haustorium induction. Ethylene inhibitors used in this study are as follows: AgNO<sub>3</sub> (Wako Chemicals), AVG (Sigma-Aldrich), AIB (Tokyo Chemical Industry), 2-picolinic acid (Sigma-Aldrich), pyrazinecarboxylic acid (Sigma-Aldrich), pyrazinamide (Sigma-Aldrich), and 1-MCP (AFxRD-0014, AgroFresh). For gas application, seedlings transferred to DMBQ medium were immediately placed inside a 9-liter plastic container followed by injection of ethylene or 1-MCP. In the case of <em>S. hermonthica</em>, germination was induced by 10 nM strigol for 24 hours, and the seedlings were then transferred to a 48-well plate containing water supplemented with DMBQ and ethylene inhibitors and incubated for 48 hours. Haustoria were observed using a stereomicroscope (Zeiss Stemi 2000-C) and a light microscope (Leica DMI3000 B). Haustorium length was determined by the distance between the haustorium tip and root surface at the opposite side of the haustorium and was measured by ImageJ software (https://imagej.nih.gov/ij/).</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Time-lapse movie of host infection</h3><p id="p-27">Surface-sterilized rice seeds (cvs Koshihikari) were germinated on the 0.8% (w/v) agar for 7 days, after which seedlings were transferred individually onto a thin layer of 0.8% (w/v) agar in a square petri dish. A 2-week-old <em>P. japonicum</em> seedling was placed next to each rice seedling, and the <em>P. japonicum</em> root tip was carefully aligned in parallel with the rice root, leaving approximately 0.5- to 1-mm distance between two roots. A cover glass was then placed on the top of the roots. The lid of the petri dish was closed and sealed with vinyl tape. Time-lapse images were taken from the bottom of the petri dish at intervals of 30 min for a period of 3 days under continuous light conditions at 25°C using a Leica M165 FC stereoscope. The images were processed into a movie using ImageJ software.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Xylem staining</h3><p id="p-28">For xylem cell staining, haustoria were excised from roots, immersed in 10% (w/v) KOH (Wako Chemicals, Osaka, Japan) for 15 min at 90°C, rinsed three times with water, immersed in 0.1% (w/v) Safranin O (Wako Chemicals, Osaka, Japan) solution for 5 min at 90°C, and rinsed three times with water. The stained haustoria were cleared by soaking samples in clearing solution [chloral hydrate (2.5 g ml<sup>−1</sup>) (Nacalai Tesque), 33% (v/v) glycerol] from 3 hours to overnight before observation. Safranin O–stained xylem cells were observed with a light microscope (Leica DMI3000 B) and an LSM700 laser-scanning confocal microscope (Carl Zeiss) with excitation and emission wavelength suitable for fluorescent images of red fluorescent protein (RFP).</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Host infection</h3><p id="p-29"><em>P. japonicum</em> seedlings were grown on growth medium for 7 days and subsequently on nutrient-free 0.8% (w/v) agar for 3 days before infection. Four-day-old <em>Arabidopsis</em> seedlings were carefully placed next to <em>P. japonicum</em> seedlings such that the <em>P. japonicum</em> root tip was touching an <em>Arabidopsis</em> root at 1 cm above the tip to allow efficient infection. One-week-old rice seedlings (cv. Koshihikari) grown vertically on 0.8% (w/v) agar medium were infected by <em>P. japonicum</em> seedlings by placing the root tip of <em>P. japonicum</em> next to a rice root. Infection was assayed on 0.8% (w/v) agar medium at 25°C with a light/dark cycle of 16 hours/8 hours. Ethylene inhibitors were added to the 0.8% agar medium during infection. To compare the same-aged haustoria in each experiment, haustoria formed at 1 day after infection were marked by mark pen on the back side of the petri dish and only marked haustoria were subjected to subsequent invasion percentage calculation. Root segments of <em>Arabidopsis</em> and <em>P. japonicum</em> contacting sites were finally excised and stained with Safranin O. Upon staining, intrusive cells showed deep red color and elongated palisade shape at the haustorium tip inside host root, and these characteristics were used as a marker for successful invasion. The invasion level was calculated by dividing the number of invaded haustoria by the total number of host-attached haustoria in each plant and shown as percentages.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Tissue sections and toluidine blue staining</h3><p id="p-30">Root segments (about 3 mm) containing haustoria were fixed, embedded in Technovit 7100 (Heraeus Kulzer), and sectioned to a 6- to 8-μm thickness by following a previous report (<a id="xref-ref-21-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a>). Sections were incubated with 0.01% (w/v) toluidine blue (Sigma-Aldrich) for 2 min, washed once with water, and observed with a light microscope (Leica DMI3000 B).</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>EdU staining</h3><p id="p-31">Haustoria induced by 10 μM DMBQ were incubated in a 10 μM EdU solution (Click-iT EdU Imaging Kits, C10337, Invitrogen) in water for 30 min at 25°C. Tissue fixation and cell permeabilization were performed according to the manufacturer’s protocols. The EdU-stained haustoria were detected with a confocal microscope (Zeiss LSM700) with excitation and emission wavelengths suitable for green fluorescent protein (GFP).</p></div><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>Plasmid construction</h3><p id="p-32">For complementation of Pj<em>ein2</em>, genomic DNA of Pj<em>EIN2</em> including the native promoter and the 3′ untranslated region (UTR) with a total length of 10,535 bp was amplified by polymerase chain reaction (PCR) using primers 5′-CTAGAGGATCCCCGGATAATAGTTGGAGGGGAAGAGTG-3′ and 5′-AAGAATTCGAGCTCGGCAGCTACCTATTGACTTACCAACA-3′, inserted into the binary vector pRedRoot that contains a constitutively expressed UBQ-DsRED construct (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44"><em>44</em></a>), and linearized by Kpn I using the In-Fusion HD Cloning Kit (Takara Bio). DsRED fluorescence was used as a visible marker for hairy root transformation. The DR5:3×VENUS-NLS construct was provided by E. Meyerowitz and described previously (<a id="xref-ref-4-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4"><em>4</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Hairy root transformation in <em>P. japonicum</em></h3><p id="p-33">All constructed plasmids were transformed into <em>Agrobacterium rhizogenes</em> strain AR1193 or LBA1334. Hairy roots were induced by <em>A. rhizogenes</em> by a previously described protocol (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45"><em>45</em></a>). Three to 4 weeks after transformation, the hairy roots together with whole plants were transferred to 0.8% (w/v) agar medium for 2 days and subjected to the haustorium induction assay on agar medium containing 10 μM DMBQ. Transformed hairy roots were selected by marker expression.</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Construction of the <em>P. japonicum</em> reference sequence</h3><p id="p-34">Genomic DNA was extracted from leaves of <em>P. japonicum</em> inbred lines grown on soil for 1 month using the cetyltrimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) method as described previously (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46"><em>46</em></a>). Briefly, the WT reference genome was sequenced with 98.3 times coverage with different insert lengths of Illumina libraries and 10.3 times coverage of PacBio sequencer long reads. The sequencing libraries were prepared using a TruSeq pair-end library kit (Illumina) for insert sizes of 180, 250, and 500 bp and a Nextera mate pair library preparation kit (Illumina) for 5-, 10-, 15-, and 20-kb mate-pair libraries according to the manufacturer’s instruction (table S1). Sequencing reactions were run with 101 or 151 cycles using either HiSeq 2000 or HiSeq 2500 sequencers. Low-quality reads of paired-end sequences were trimmed and filtered using the FASTX toolkit (<a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/</a>) with fastq_quality trimmer function with -1 125 –t 30 options. For mate-pair library sequences, the sequence reads were trimmed to 50-bp length with the fastx_trimmer function, followed by the fastq_quality_trimmer with option –l 45 and –t 30. Quality-filtered reads were assembled with allpaths-lg (version R48894) (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47"><em>47</em></a>). The PacBio libraries were constructed with DNA template prep kit 2.0 (3 to 10 kb) and sequenced using C2 chemistry with standard or XL polymerase. The sequence reads were error-collected with the program sprai (<a href="https://anaconda.org/bioconda/sprai">https://anaconda.org/bioconda/sprai</a>) and gap-filled the allpaths-lg assembled genome using PBJelly (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48"><em>48</em></a>). Genome annotation was carried out using MAKER-P software (version 2.27) (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49"><em>49</em></a>). At first, a repeat library was constructed and repeat sequences were masked using LTRharvest (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50"><em>50</em></a>), MITE-Hunter (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51"><em>51</em></a>), and Repeat modelar (Smit, AFA, Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. 2008-2015; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). Transcriptome data obtained from 1 and 7 days after rice infection in rhizotron systems were mapped to the genome assembly by TopHat2 (version 2.0.13) (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52"><em>52</em></a>), and the alignments were used as a training dataset for AUGUSTUS (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53"><em>53</em></a>) and GeneMark-ET (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54"><em>54</em></a>). Putative gene function was assigned using the Trinotate program (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55"><em>55</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-22" class="subsection"><h3>Whole-genome resequencing of the mutant populations</h3><p id="p-35">For Pj<em>ein2</em>, a single leaf from each of 50 plants of an M5 homozygous mutant line and 50 plants of F2 plants with the elongated haustorium phenotype after one backcross were harvested and combined to make a Pj<em>ein2</em> M pool and a BC1F2 pool, respectively. For Pj<em>etr1</em>, leaves from 25 plants with the elongated haustorium phenotype from M4, M5, BC1F3, and BC1F5 progenies were harvested to make a Pj<em>etr1</em> M/F pool. These plants are potentially a mixture of homozygous and heterozygous genotypes. Genomic DNA was extracted from each pool using the CTAB method. Paired-end Illumina libraries were prepared using a TruSeq library preparation kit with an average insert size of approximately 550 bp. Sequencing reactions of both ends were run for 251 cycles using HiSeq 2500 sequencers.</p></div><div id="sec-23" class="subsection"><h3>Identification of SNPs for Pj<em>etr1</em> and Pj<em>ein2</em></h3><p id="p-36">CLC Genomics Workbench software (version 8) was used for quality filtering, mapping to reference, and detection of SNPs. Four samples of pooled DNA were analyzed, of which the Pj<em>etr1</em> M/F pool, the Pj<em>ein2</em> M pool, and the Pj<em>ein2</em> BC1F2 pool were prepared as described above. The remaining group was derived from an unrelated mutant, where the DNA was extracted from 50 mutant pools of F2 progeny and designated as an “unrelated mutant pool” (see also figs. S2 and S5) to use for nonspecific background SNP subtraction. Quality of reads was checked by FastQC (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56"><em>56</em></a>). Reads with a quality score lower than 0.05 and lengths shorter than 50 bp were removed and further trimmed by removing five nucleotides from the 5′ end and 70 nucleotides from the 3′ end, resulting in average read lengths of 175 bp. Paired reads from four groups were mapped to <em>P. japonicum</em> WT reference genome assembly version 1 with parameters of mismatch cost at 2, length fraction at 0.6, and similarity fraction at 0.9. Low-stringency SNPs in each of four groups were called with parameters of minimum coverage 2, minimum count 2, and minimum frequency 10% and used for removing background SNPs across different groups through comparison. High-stringency SNPs in the Pj<em>ein2</em> M pool and the BC1F2M/F pool were called with parameters of required variant probability 90%, minimum frequency 90%, minimum coverage 5, and minimum count 5. The same parameters were applied to high-stringency SNPs in the Pj<em>etr1</em> M/F pool except for a minimum frequency at 40% because collected plants are potentially mixtures of homozygous and heterozygous plants. Background SNPs (noise) were removed by subtracting each of the high-stringency SNPs with low-stringency SNPs appearing in other remaining groups. The resulting SNPs were considered as “unique SNPs” and subsequently annotated onto transcripts in the reference <em>P. japonicum</em> genome to analyze nonsynonymous mutations (nonsynonymous SNPs).</p></div><div id="sec-24" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic tree construction</h3><p id="p-37">The amino acid sequences from each species were obtained from phytozome (V12.1). <em>P. japonicum</em> orthologous genes were identified from reciprocal blast against <em>Arabidopsis</em> genes. The full-length amino acid sequences were aligned by ClustalW (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57"><em>57</em></a>). Maximum likelihood phylogenetic trees were estimated by RAxML software (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58"><em>58</em></a>) using GAMMAAUTO model. Bootstrap values were calculated as percentage of 1000 repetitions.</p></div></div><div class="section app" id="app-1"><h2 class="">SUPPLEMENTARY MATERIALS</h2><div id="DC1" class="supplementary-material"><p id="p-38">Supplementary material for this article is available at <a href="http://advances.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/6/44/eabc2385/DC1">http://advances.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/6/44/eabc2385/DC1</a></p></div></div><div class="section app" id="app-2"><p id="p-39"><a href="https://en.bio-protocol.org/cjrap.aspx?eid=10.1126/sciadv.abc2385">View/request a protocol for this paper from <em>Bio-protocol</em></a>.</p></div><div class="license" <span class="cit-article-title">Local auxin biosynthesis mediated by a YUCCA flavin monooxygenase regulates haustorium development in the parasitic plant <em>Phtheirospermum japonicum</em></span>. <abbr class="cit-jnl-abbrev">Plant Cell</abbr> 
<span class="cit-vol">28</span>, 
<span class="cit-fpage">1795</span>–<span class="cit-lpage">1814</span> (<span class="cit-pub-date">2016</span>).</cite></div><div class="cit-extra"><a href="{openurl}?query=rft.jtitle%253DPlant%2BCell%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Adoi%252F10.1105%252Ftpc.16.00310%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Apmid%252F27385817%26rft.genre%253Darticle%26rft_val_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Ajournal%26ctx_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ctx_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Actx" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-openurl cit-ref-sprinkles-open-url"><span>OpenUrl</span></a><a href="/lookup/ijlink/YTozOntzOjQ6InBhdGgiO3M6MTQ6Ii9sb29rdXAvaWpsaW5rIjtzOjU6InF1ZXJ5IjthOjQ6e3M6ODoibGlua1R5cGUiO3M6NDoiQUJTVCI7czoxMToiam91cm5hbENvZGUiO3M6OToicGxhbnRjZWxsIjtzOjU6InJlc2lkIjtzOjk6IjI4LzgvMTc5NSI7czo0OiJhdG9tIjtzOjI4OiIvYWR2YW5jZXMvNi80NC9lYWJjMjM4NS5hdG9tIjt9czo4OiJmcmFnbWVudCI7czowOiIiO30=" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-ijlink"><span><span class="cit-reflinks-abstract">Abstract</span><span class="cit-sep cit-reflinks-variant-name-sep">/</span><span class="cit-reflinks-full-text"><span class="free-full-text">FREE </span>Full Text</span></span></a></div></div></li><li><a class="rev-xref-ref" href="#xref-ref-5-1" title="View reference 5 in text" id="ref-5">↵</a><div class="cit ref-cit ref-journal" id="cit-6.44.eabc2385.5" data-doi="10.1242/dev.164848"><div class="cit-metadata"><ol class="cit-auth-list"><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">T.</span> <span class="cit-name-surname">Wakatake</span></span>, </li><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">S.</span> <span class="cit-name-surname">Yoshida</span></span>, </li><li><span class="cit-auth"> <span class="cit-name-given-names">K.</span> <span class="cit-name-surname">Shirasu</span></span></li></ol><cite>,

<span class="cit-article-title">RAxML version 8: A tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies</span>. <abbr class="cit-jnl-abbrev">Bioinformatics</abbr> 
<span class="cit-vol">30</span>, 
<span class="cit-fpage">1312</span>–<span class="cit-lpage">1313</span> (<span class="cit-pub-date">2014</span>).</cite></div><div class="cit-extra"><a href="{openurl}?query=rft.jtitle%253DBioinformatics%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Adoi%252F10.1093%252Fbioinformatics%252Fbtu033%26rft_id%253Dinfo%253Apmid%252F24451623%26rft.genre%253Darticle%26rft_val_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Ajournal%26ctx_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ver%253DZ39.88-2004%26url_ctx_fmt%253Dinfo%253Aofi%252Ffmt%253Akev%253Amtx%253Actx" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-openurl cit-ref-sprinkles-open-url"><span>OpenUrl</span></a><a href="/lookup/external-ref?access_num=10.1093/bioinformatics/btu033&amp;link_type=DOI" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-doi cit-ref-sprinkles-crossref"><span>CrossRef</span></a><a href="/lookup/external-ref?access_num=24451623&amp;link_type=MED&amp;atom=%2Fadvances%2F6%2F44%2Feabc2385.atom" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-medline"><span>PubMed</span></a><a href="/lookup/external-ref?access_num=000336095100024&amp;link_type=ISI" class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-newisilink cit-ref-sprinkles-webofscience"><span>Web of Science</span></a></div></div></li></ol></div><div class="ack"><strong>Acknowledgments: </strong>We thank Y. Yoshimura for technical assistance, M. Kasahara at Tokyo University for helpful advice, AgroFresh for providing 1-MCP, Y. Saijo at the Nara Institute of Science and Technology for providing Arabidopsis ethylene-related mutants, A. G. T. Babiker for providing <em>Striga</em> seeds, K. Mori for providing strigol, and E. Meyerowitz for providing the <em>DR5:3×VENUS-NLS</em> construct. Computations were partially performed on the NIG supercomputer at the ROIS National Institute of Genetics. <strong>Funding:</strong> This research was partially supported by MEXT KAKENHI grants (nos. 17K1542 to S.C.; 22128008 to T.N.; 22128001 to M.H.; 15H01246, 25711019, 18H02464, and 18H04838 to S.Y.; and 15H05959 and 17H06172 to K.S.), JST PRESTO (JPMJPR194D to S.Y.), and MEXT/JSPS KAKENHI grant numbers 221S0002 and 16H06279 (PAGS). <strong>Author contributions:</strong> S.C., K.S., and S.Y. conceived the project. S.Y. isolated the original mutants and assembled and annotated the genome. T.N., T.F.S., S.S., A.T., and M.H. prepared the sequence library and sequenced the genome. J.K.I. conducted RNA sequencing for genome annotation. T.K. performed the EdU experiments. S.C. performed the SNP analysis, cloned the genes, and conducted the remaining experiments. S.C. prepared figures and tables. S.C., S.Y., and K.S. wrote and revised the manuscript. All authors read and revised the manuscript. <strong>Competing interests:</strong> The authors declare that they have no competing interests. <strong>Data and materials availability:</strong> The plant materials used in this study are available from the corresponding author upon reasonable requests. Sequence reads generated in this study have been deposited in DDBJ with accession numbers DRA003500, DRA010352, and DRA010387 for WT reference genome and DRA010647 for Pj<em>etr1</em> M/F, Pj<em>ein2</em> M5, and Pj<em>ein2</em>BC1F2 pools. Genome assembly and annotation are deposited to DDBJ with accession numbers BMAC01000001–BMAC01010559 and also available at Dryad database (<a href="https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.vt4b8gtpt">https://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.vt4b8gtpt</a>). Additional data are available from authors upon request.</div><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">Copyright © 2020 The Authors, some rights reserved; exclusive licensee American Association for the Advancement of Science.         <h3 class="highwire-cite-title-wrapper media__headline"><a href="/content/6/44/eabc2385/suppl/DC1" class="highwire-cite-linked-title" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="highwire-cite-title media__headline__title">Ethylene signaling mediates host invasion by parasitic plants</div></a></h3>
            <p  class="highwire-cite-authors byline">By <span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Songkui Cui</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Tomoya Kubota</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="2">Tomoaki Nishiyama</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="3">Juliane K.             <p  class="highwire-cite-metadata byline"><em>Science Advances</em><time>28 Oct 2020</time> : eabc2385 </p>

    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="241928" id="node241928--2" data-pisa="advances;6/44/eabc2385" data-pisa-master="advances;6/44/eabc2385" data-apath="/advances/6/44/eabc2385.atom"><article class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-jnl-sci-toc clearfix media media--citation">
    <div class="media__body">
        <h3 class="highwire-cite-title-wrapper media__headline"><a href="/content/6/44/eabc2385/suppl/DC1" class="highwire-cite-linked-title" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="highwire-cite-title media__headline__title">Ethylene signaling mediates host invasion by parasitic plants</div></a></h3>
            <p  class="highwire-cite-authors byline">By <span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Songkui Cui</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Tomoya Kubota</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="2">Tomoaki Nishiyama</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="3">Juliane K. Ishida</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="4">Shuji Shigenobu</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="5">Tomoko F. Shibata</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="6">Atsushi Toyoda</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="7">Mitsuyasu Hasebe</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="8">Ken Shirasu</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="9">Satoko Yoshida</span></span></p>
            <p  class="highwire-cite-metadata byline"><em>Science Advances</em><time>28 Oct 2020</time> : eabc2385 </p>
                <div class="highwire-cite-snippet media__deck">
      <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="section precis" id="abstract-1" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><p id="p-3">Forward genetic analysis in a parasitic plant revealed that ethylene signaling is essential for host invasion.</p></div></div>    </div>
        <ul class="variant-list media__links"><li class="supplement-data first last"><a href="/content/6/44/eabc2385/suppl/DC1" class="highwire-variant-link variant-supplemental-material" title="Supplementary Materials" rel="alternate" type="text/html" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Supplementary Materials</a></li>
</ul>  </div>
</article></div>  </div>
